    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="596f49d6db8c4729848ef75bde0fa197"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="596f49d6db8c4729848ef75bde0fa197">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '1632f1d6-c4fd-4c17-a00d-ecbed45403f9'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Xunhui Xiong</p>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">
                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:esxxiong@scut.edu.cn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>esxxiong@scut.edu.cn</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">
                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a target="_blank" class="sm-account__link" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-5858-9247"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-orcid"></i><span>orcid.org/0000-0002-5858-9247</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>School of Environment and Energy, South China University of Technology, Guangzhou, 510006 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Xiong/Xunhui">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d82955984" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A steric hindrance strategy has been proposed to modulate the closed pores formation of polymer-derived hard carbon (HC). Sterically hindered groups with aromatic ring are crucial to enhance the rigidity and internal free volumes of polymer, which could suppress the polymer rearrangements and induce the formation of close pores. Moreover, the fabricated HC anode exhibit excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity and fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport for sodium-ion battery.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a75812b2-c8d9-440d-a892-9a49e1d76637/anie202409906-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a75812b2-c8d9-440d-a892-9a49e1d76637/anie202409906-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/25fdbacb-3e01-4b08-853b-69186cc795d1/anie202409906-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a75812b2-c8d9-440d-a892-9a49e1d76637/anie202409906-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d82956022" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The closed pores play a critical role in improving the sodium storage capacity of hard carbon (HC) anode, however, their formation mechanism as well as the efficient modulation strategy at molecular level in the polymer-derived HCs is still lacking. In this work, the steric hindrance effect has been proposed to create closed pores in the polymer-derived HCs for the first time through grafting the aromatic rings within and between the main chains in the precursor. The experimental data and theoretical calculation demonstrate that steric-hindrance effect from the aromatic ring side group can increase backbone rigidity and the internal free volumes in the polymer precursor, which can prevent the over graphitization and facilitate the formation of closed pores during the carbonization process. As a result, the as-prepared HC anode exhibits a remarkably enhanced discharge capacity of 340.3 mAh/g at 0.1 C, improved rate performance (210.7 mAh/g at 5 C) as well as boosted cycling stability (86.4 % over 1000 cycles at 2 C). This work provides a new insight into the formation mechanisms of closed pores via steric hindrance engineering, which can shed light on the development of high-performance polymer-derived HC anode for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202409906-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202409906-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as a competitive candidate to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to their similar working mechanisms as well as the cost-effective sodium resources, and therefore the development of SIBs technology to meet the increasing demand for large-scale energy storage systems has become a significant task.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0001_R_d82955976e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> In the past decade, SIBs technology has witnessed a few milestones, but some critical performance indicators of SIBs are still striving to satisfy the commercial requirements. On the anode side, the main challenge lies in the development of the appropriate materials to accommodate Na<sup>+</sup> with a larger ionic radius, since the commercial graphite anode exhibits a very poor sodium storage performance mainly because of the thermodynamic limitation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0004_R_d82955976e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span> Hard carbon (HC), which consists of randomly oriented graphite regions and turbostratic nanodomains with internal micropores, can provide abundant defects, favorable interlayer spacing (&gt;0.37 nm) and substantial nanovoids for sodium storage sites.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0005_R_d82955976e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett.">3</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, the simple fabrication process as well as the low operation voltage has enabled HC to become the most promising anode candidate for SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0006_R_d82955976e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span> Even though the sodium storage mechanism in HC is ongoing debates, the plateau capacity below 0.1 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), which contributes the main sodium storage capacity, is highly related with the closed pores in HC.</p>
         
         <p>The rational modification of the precursors of HCs has been proved to be an efficient strategy to modulate the formation of closed pores.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0007_R_d82955976e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - ACS Appl. Energ. Mater.">5</a></sup></span> Among different precursors to fabricate HCs anode, the synthetic polymer precursors have gained great attentions owing to their relatively high carbon yield as well as the highly controllable microstructure of the final HC anode by optimizing the cross-linking degree of the polymer.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0008_R_d82955976e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Energy Lett.">6</a></sup></span> For example, triazine and carbonyl groups have been proposed to crosslink with polystyrene nanospheres to create abundant closed pores in the derived HC anode, which showed much higher sodium storage capacity when compared with pristine polystyrene-derived HC.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0009_R_d82955976e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Carbon Energy.">7</a></sup></span> However, the polymer-derived HCs usually delivered insufficient reversible capacities.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0010_R_d82955976e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> Thereafter, different pore-forming strategies, mainly through chemical-activation or template agents, have been proposed during the carbonization process of polymer precursors. For example, ZnO bulk etching has been proved to be beneficial to the formation of micropores in the resin-derived HC, which can offer a high reversible capacity of 501 mAh/g.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0013_R_d82955976e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span> Ethanol steam vapor can create pore cavities among cross-linked phenol-formaldehyde resin during the solvothermal process and they can be converted into closed pores in the following high-temperature carbonization process, which can boost the Na storage capacity to 410 mAh/g.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0014_R_d82955976e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Energy Lett.">10</a></sup></span> In addition, poly (vinyl butyral),<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0015_R_d82955976e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Appl. Energ. Mater.">11</a></sup></span> CO<sub>2</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0016_R_d82955976e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span> KOH<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0017_R_d82955976e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span> were also utilized to construct the closed pores in polymer-derived HCs. However, these pioneering strategies still fail to reveal the formation mechanism of closed pores from the polymer precursor at molecular level.</p>
         
         <p>The steric-hindrance effect induced by the structural modifications has showed considerable potentials to address multi-domain issues including organic light-emitting diode,<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0018_R_d82955976e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">14</a></sup></span> gas separation,<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0019_R_d82955976e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Ind. Eng. Chem. Res.">15</a></sup></span> photodynamic therapy,<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0020_R_d82955976e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16">16</a></sup></span> and so on. More importantly, the steric-hindrance effect not only can tune the stacking mode of macromolecules, but also restrict the intramolecular motion to reconstruct the spatial conformation of polymer.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0020_R_d82955976e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16">16</a></sup></span> Thus, the rational design of synthetic polymer precursors through steric-hindrance engineering may be a promising strategy to control the pore structure and reveal the formation mechanism of closed pores of polymer-derive HC, which has rarely been reported though it shows superior repeatability and high product stability.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, the steric-hindrance effect has been introduced into phenolic resin precursor during the polymerization reaction between aldehydes and resorcinol to facilitate the formation of closed pores in the final HC anode. The experimental data and theoretical calculation demonstrate that the steric-hindrance effect arising from aromatic ring in aldehyde can greatly increase the free volume and backbone rigidity in the phenolic resin, which can reduce the number of polymer rearrangements and avoid the over graphitization during the carbonization process. Thanks to the abundant closed pores via the steric-hindrance strategy, the as-prepared HC anode can show an enhanced capacity of 340.3 mAh/g with remarkably high and reversible plateau capacity, superior rate capability (210.7 mAh/g at 5 C) as well as excellent cycling performance over 1000 cycles at 2 C. Our work provides new insights into the closed pores formation in the polymer-derived HC, which paves another potential strategy to design and prepare high-performance HC anode for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202409906-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202409906-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0001">1a</a> and Figure S1 (Supporting Information), cinnamaldehyde with styryl group has been used to polymerize with resorcinol to introduce the steric hindrance into the phenolic resin precursor (denoted as RFSH). For comparison, traditional resorcinol/formaldehyde resin precursor (denoted as RF) has also been prepared via the similar crosslink reaction. The fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectrum was firstly performed to investigate the chemical structure of as-prepared RFSH and RF precursors. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0001">1b</a> and Table S2 (Supporting Information), both RFSH and RF polymers show obvious peaks at 1000–1180 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1380–1520 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can be assigned to the newly formed −C−O−C− and −CH− linkages between the resorcinol rings, respectively, indicating the occurrence of the condensation reaction between resorcinol and aldehyde.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0023_R_d82955976e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater.">17</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, the characteristic bands of monosubstituted benzene ring (denoted as Ph−H, 699 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and =CH− group (2970–3120 cm<sup>−1</sup>) can be only observed in the RFSH polymer, confirming that styryl group in aldehyde has been introduced into RFSH precursor in the crosslink reaction.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0024_R_d82955976e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18">18</a></sup></span> More structural information of RFSH and RF can be obtained in the <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectrum (Figure S2, Supporting Information). Board signals corresponding to −CH− linkages units are emerged in RFSH and RF in the region of 5.3–5.8 and 3.9–5.8 ppm, respectively, which are consistent with the FTIR results.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0027_R_d82955976e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Polym. Eng. Sci.">19</a></sup></span> The appearance of resonance signal of Ph−H units (the H on the monosubstituted benzene ring were denoted as h, i, j, k, l, respectively) at 7.5–7.8 ppm indicates that phenyls are grafted in the RFSH polymer framework.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0028_R_d82955976e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">20</a></sup></span> The insoluble fraction (IF) test in ethanol was performed to evaluate the different crosslink-reaction degrees of RF and RFSH precursor. As shown in Figure S3 (Supporting Information), the IF value of RFSH is lower than that of RF suggesting that the cross-linking degree of the RFSH is lower than RF, which can be attributed to the steric strain barriers of styryl group in cinnamaldehyde.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0029_R_d82955976e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nat. Commun.">21</a></sup></span> Consequently, the RFSH precursor has been successfully synthesized via the condensation reaction between resorcinol and cinnamaldehyde.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202409906-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/246a6bdf-460b-4ee7-abc7-ff92d0090b21/anie202409906-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/246a6bdf-460b-4ee7-abc7-ff92d0090b21/anie202409906-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2cddaf57-8e01-44da-9d36-b7de41337924/anie202409906-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/246a6bdf-460b-4ee7-abc7-ff92d0090b21/anie202409906-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202409906-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202409906" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Scheme of synthesis process of RFSH and RF precursors; (b) FTIR spectrum, (c) XRD patterns and (d) Compressive strain-stress curves for RFSH and RF.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The effect of different side groups in the aldehyde on the packing behavior of the final polymer precursors was then explored by X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0001">1c</a>), in which the broad peaks in the 2θ range of 15–25° illustrate their amorphous characteristics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0019_R_d82955976e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Ind. Eng. Chem. Res.">15</a></sup></span> However, it can be calculated that RFSH shows a larger average interchain distance than that of RF (4.9 Å <i>vs</i>. 4.6 Å) according to interlayer spacing calculation, which suggests that more significant disruption of chain packing is observed in RFSH precursor caused by the introduction of the styryl group. Moreover, the full width at half maximum (FWHM) of RFSH is smaller than that of RF, which can be attributed to the π–π stacking of the aromatic rings within and between the main chains.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0030_R_d82955976e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">22</a></sup></span> It has been reported that the outward extension of the phenyls on the −CH− linkages can provide substantial steric hindrance and π–π stacking, and then can further improve interchain distance and the chain rigidity, which can also be verified by the mechanical compression result for RFSH and RF precursors.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0031_R_d82955976e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23">23</a></sup></span> As displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0001">1d</a>, the RFSH exhibits a higher compression modulus of 10.2 MPa than that of the RF (5.4 MPa), estimated by the ratio compressive stress (σ)/strain (ϵ), indicating that the π–π stacking of sterically hindered phenyls can greatly enhance the rigidity of RFSH macromolecules, which can further restrict disentanglement and polymer chains movement under compression. Fortunately, the superior rigidity of RFSH backbone could be beneficial to suppress the polymer rearrangements and prevent the over graphitization during the following carbonization process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0034_R_d82955976e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Macromolecules.">24</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To investigate the effect of different side groups in the aldehyde on the free volumes in the final precursors, the molecular dynamics simulations have also performed. As displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0002">2a</a> and <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0002">2b</a>, the free volumes of RFSH and RF, defined as the blue areas, were visualized and calculated using Materials Studio (MS). According to the calculation results in Table S3 (Supporting Information), the free volumes in RFSH are larger than that in RF, which is largely because the styryl group in RFSH can effectively prevent polymer chain packing during the polymerization reaction. The similar result can also be observed in the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption tests for RFSH and RF precursors. As shown in Figure S4 and Table S4 (Supporting Information), the BET surface area and pore volume of RFSH are six times higher than those of RF, which can facilitate the formation of abundant pores in the final HC product during the carbonization process.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202409906-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/04550aed-3c92-447c-825d-82167900fece/anie202409906-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/04550aed-3c92-447c-825d-82167900fece/anie202409906-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/28cd6f3b-3d08-46b9-9ca4-9bdb31b16329/anie202409906-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/04550aed-3c92-447c-825d-82167900fece/anie202409906-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202409906-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202409906" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Computational simulation model of free volumes of (a) RF and (b) RFSH; (c) TGA curves and (d) DTG curves of RFSH and RF; (e) Ex situ FTIR spectrums of RFSH at 25 °C, 400 °C, 600 °C and 800 °C; (f) Possible carbonization mechanism of RFSH.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) and ex situ FTIR spectroscopy were then carried out to study the HC formation mechanisms from RFSH and RF precursors. In TGA curve (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0002">2c</a>), the notably different carbon yields (47.5 % <i>vs</i>. 56.6 %) as well as the onset temperature of weight stabilization for RFSH and RF precursor (712 °C vs. 966 °C) indicate their different HC formation mechanism, which can be more clearly observed in the derivative thermogravimetry (DTG) curves. As displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0002">2d</a>, the weight loss below ~220 °C is mainly attributed to the release of absorbed water and small molecules in both RFSH and RF precursor. Meanwhile, the maximum weight-loss rate for RFSH and RF occurred at ~345 °C is related to the pyrolysis of the unstable aliphatic chains (−CH−, =CH−, etc), which can be proved by the disappearance of their characteristic peaks in the ex situ FTIR spectra of the precursors after annealing at 400 °C (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0002">2e</a> and Figure S5, Supporting Information).<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0035_R_d82955976e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - SusMat.">25</a></sup></span> Notably, the cracking aliphatic chains mainly include −CH− bond between resorcinol and extra alkene (such as −CH=CH−) from the grafted styryl group for RFSH, and only −CH− linkages can be broken for RF in this temperature range, and therefore the weight loss of RFSH is higher than that of RF during the pyrolysis process. However, the peak of phenyls (~699 cm<sup>−1</sup>) on the −CH− linkages still exists in RFSH after annealing at 400 °C, indicating the higher thermal stability of phenyls in RFSH, which is beneficial to form a stable carbon skeleton in the subsequent carbonization process. With the increase of annealing temperatures, the DTG curves also show striking differences with two peaks at 486 °C and 882 °C for RF and no peaks in the temperature range of 345–1000 °C for RFSH precursor. To understand the differences, the FTIR curves of RFSH and RF precursors annealed at 600 °C and 800 °C were collected. After annealing at 600 °C the formation of trisubstituted benzene ring (879 cm<sup>−1</sup>) in both RF and RFSH precursors is occurred with the greatly increased peak intensity of −CH− groups at ~1460 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can be attributed to the cross-linking reactions between aliphatic chains and phenyls or resorcinol in carbon matrix.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0036_R_d82955976e641" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span> However, the cross-linking process in RFSH can facilitate more aliphatic and phenyls to be stable carbon skeletons. As a result, no sharp increase of weight loss is happened in RFSH at 486 °C, while an obvious DTG peak of RF can be observed at this temperature. When the annealing temperature was increased to 800 °C, RFSH exhibits the similar FTIR spectroscopy with that at 600 °C, suggesting a stable carbon skeleton has formed at this temperature range, which agrees well with the very low mass loss in the ranges 600–800 °C in TGA analysis. However, the intensity of the −CH− vibration peak (~1460 cm<sup>−1</sup>) in RF decreases obviously at 800 °C with a mass loss of 6.1 % from 600 °C to 800 °C, indicating the cleavage of −CH− chains accompanied with extra structural rearrangement process is still undergoing. Based on the above analysis, the formation mechanism of RFSH-derived HC can be described as “dehydration-crosslinking-aromatization” process, while RF-derived HC can be illustrated as “dehydration-crosslinking-rearrangement-aromatization” process, as displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0002">2f</a> and Figure S6 (Supporting Information). The result indicates that the rigid skeleton of RFSH constructed by steric hindrance can effectively prevent the cleavage rearrangement, which is beneficial to the formation of short-range carbon layer during pyrolysis process.</p>
         
         <p>To obtain HCs powders, RFSH and RF precursors were carbonized at 1400 °C, and the as-obtained samples were denoted as RFSH-1400 and RF-1400, respectively. As shown in the SEM images in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0003">3a</a>–<a href="#anie202409906-fig-0003">b</a>, both RFSH-1400 and RF-1400 samples exhibit irregular particles with a size of 20–40 μm. The transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0003">3c</a>–<a href="#anie202409906-fig-0003">f</a> show that RFSH-1400 contains highly disordered and distorted graphite-like structure with a large number of short-range carbon layers, whereas RF-1400 is mainly composed of long-range ordered graphitic-like domains. More importantly, RFSH-1400 possesses abundant closed pores, which are constructed by short carbon layers walls (marked by the red dashed line in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0003">3e</a>). In sharp comparison, mesopores can be observed in RF-1400 (marked by the red dashed line in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0003">3f</a>). The different pore structure in RFSH-1400 and RF-1400 can be verified by the small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0003">3g</a>, both RFSH-1400 and RF-1400 samples exhibit a plateau of higher scattering intensity, which is associated with the well-developed internal closed-pore structure.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0037_R_d82955976e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span> However, the RFSH-1400 possesses a more obvious peak around 0.1 Å<sup>−1</sup>, signifying a higher content of closed pores than RF-1400. All these phenomena demonstrate the steric hindrance arising from the aromatic ring in the RFSH precursor can effectively prevent the excessive rearrangement of the carbon layers and transfer the free volume into closed pores during the carbonization process (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0003">3h</a>).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202409906-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4520ea72-7690-4a81-84aa-772f5691d22b/anie202409906-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4520ea72-7690-4a81-84aa-772f5691d22b/anie202409906-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/02910001-c779-4e8a-980b-76e9d6878018/anie202409906-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4520ea72-7690-4a81-84aa-772f5691d22b/anie202409906-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202409906-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202409906" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>SEM images of (a) RFSH-1400 and (b) RF-1400; TEM and HRTEM images of (c, e) RFSH-1400 and (d, f) RF-1400; (g) SAXS patterns of RF-1400 and RFSH-1400; (h) Schematic illustration of the microstructure of RFSH-1400 and RF-1400.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>XRD patterns and Raman spectroscopy were performed to obtain more structural information of different HC samples. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0004">4a</a>, XRD patterns of RFSH-1400 and RF-1400 show two broad peaks at 20–30° and 40–50°, corresponding to the (002) and (100) crystal planes of amorphous carbon, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0038_R_d82955976e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a></sup></span> The (002) diffraction peak of RFSH-1400 shifts to a lower degree, implying that the steric-hindrance effect in the precursor leads to a larger interlayer for RFSH-1400. Specifically, it can be calculated that RFSH-1400 exhibits a larger interlayer spacing of 0.398 nm than that of RF-1400 (0.373 nm), which can be also observed in the HRTEM images (Figure S7, Supporting Information). The larger interlayer spacing can facilitate efficient the intercalation/deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> during the charge/ discharge processes. Raman spectroscopy demonstrates that the integral intensity ratio A<sub>D</sub>/A<sub>G</sub> of RFSH-1400 is larger than that of RF-1400 (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0004">4b</a> and Figure S8, Supporting Information), implying a more disordered microstructure for RFSH-1400 sample.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0039_R_d82955976e723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span> In addition, it can be calculated from Raman that the carbon layers in RFSH-1400 exhibit smaller lateral width (L<sub>a</sub>) than that of RF-1400 (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0004">4c</a> and Table S5, Supporting Information). The shorter or more curved carbon layers in RFSH-1400 are beneficial to construct abundant closed pores during the carbonization process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0040_R_d82955976e731" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Sustain. Mater. Technol.">30</a></sup></span> X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis shows that no other heteroatom element, except for C, H and O atoms, can be detected in two kinds of HC samples (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0004">4d</a> and Figure S9, Supporting Information). It is worth noting that the sp<sup>3</sup> hybridized carbon content of RFSH-1400 is significantly higher than that of RF-1400, signifying that steric-hindered styryl group in RFSH-1400 can bring in more disorder structure or defects during the pyrolysis process. Furthermore, the less ordering of the graphitic domains with higher sp<sup>3</sup> carbon can cause wider distribution spacing in RFSH-1400, which is favorable for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion during the charge/discharge processes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0037_R_d82955976e744" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span> N<sub>2</sub> physisorption tests (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0004">4e</a>–<a href="#anie202409906-fig-0004">f</a>, and Table S6, Supporting Information) reveal RFSH-1400 possesses a very low specific surface area of 0.85 m<sup>2</sup>/g and a low content of micropores and mesopores. The low open surface porosity can inhibit the contact of the electrolyte with the inner carbon surface, resulting in high initial coulombic efficiency (ICE).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202409906-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e898100d-a36b-49e7-ac70-e69a10064cdc/anie202409906-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e898100d-a36b-49e7-ac70-e69a10064cdc/anie202409906-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2ce2f4f3-eb12-4083-a168-63decbc5956f/anie202409906-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e898100d-a36b-49e7-ac70-e69a10064cdc/anie202409906-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202409906-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202409906" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) The XRD patterns, (b) Raman spectra, (c) The fitted value of L<sub>a</sub> and L<sub>c</sub>, (d) The high-resolution C 1s XPS spectra, (e) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms and (f) The pore size distribution of RFSH-1400 and RF-1400 samples.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To demonstrate the advantages of steric hindrance in polymer precursor, the sodium storage performances of RFSH-1400 and RF-1400 electrodes were investigated by assembling half-cell SIBs in both ether-based and ester-based electrolytes. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0005">5a</a> and <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0005">5b</a>, the discharge curve displays a typical voltage profile of HC anode, which consists of a conspicuous slope region above 0.1 V and plateau region closed to the 0 V. Compared with RF-1400 electrode, RFSH-1400 delivers a much higher initial reversible capacity at 0.1 C in ether-based or ester-based electrolyte (1 C=300 mA/g). As shown in Figure S10 and Table S7 (Supporting Information), the higher capacity in RFSH-1400 is mainly contributed by the plateau capacity (241.6 mAh/g in ether and 243.1 mAh/g in ester), whereas RF-1400 exhibits a low plateau capacity value (218.8 mAh/g in ether and 205.1 mAh/g in ester). Such results directly reveal that the steric hindrance effect in the polymer precursor can induce more closed pores in RFSH-1400 sample. As a result, RFSH-1400 anode also shows a much higher ICE, which can also be confirmed by their typical cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves (Figure S11, Supporting Information). The high surface area of RF-1400 will consume more electrolyte and Na<sup>+</sup> to form solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI), leading to large inconsistencies between the first and second CV curve. Besides, the RFSH-1400 anode shows better compatibility with ester-based electrolyte than RF-1400 anode. Compared with RF-1400 anode, the reversible charge capacity and ICE of RFSH-1400 anode in ester-based electrolyte are close to those in the ether-based electrolyte, indicating that the small-sized nanopores in RFSH-1400 anode tend to be more densely stacked than RF-1400 anode, resulting in similar initial sodiation/desodiation process in two kinds of electrolytes.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202409906-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0cad77ba-f495-4a72-bcf0-ea35d8bd6121/anie202409906-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0cad77ba-f495-4a72-bcf0-ea35d8bd6121/anie202409906-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ded94851-45ae-401d-a050-fd616834e66c/anie202409906-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0cad77ba-f495-4a72-bcf0-ea35d8bd6121/anie202409906-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202409906-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202409906" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The initial discharge/charge curves of RFSH-1400 and RF-1400 in (a) ether-based electrolyte and (b) ester-based electrolyte; (c) The rate performance; Cycling performance at (d) 0.5 C and (e) 2 C of RFSH-1400 and RF-1400 in ether-based electrolyte; (f) The GITT curves and (g) Nyquist plots of RFSH-1400 and RF-1400; (h) Scheme of sodium-ion diffusion pathway in RF-1400 and RFSH-1400.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Except for the enhanced sodium storage capacity, RFSH-1400 anode exhibits much improved rate performance with a high specific capacity of 210.7 mAh/g at 5 C in ether-based electrolyte, while RF-1400 anode only delivers a low capacity of 79.5 mAh/g (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0005">5c</a>). Furthermore, the cycling stabilities of RFSH-1400 and RF-1400 electrodes were further measured and compared. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0005">5d</a>, RFSH-1400 anode can keep a capacity retention of 94.9 % after 250 cycles at 0.5 C, while RF-1400 anode maintains a capacity retention ratio of 91.8 %. However, the differences of their cycle stabilities are more obviously at larger current densities (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0005">5e</a> and Figure S12, Supporting Information). At a current density of 2 C, RFSH-1400 electrode delivers a capacity of 252.2 mAh/g with a capacity retention of 86.4 % after 1000 cycles, while a large capacity loss of RF-1400 can be observed from 220.5 mAh/g to 168.1 mAh/g. The superior cycle stability of RFSH-1400 anode can also be confirmed by the cycle performance in ester-based electrolyte (Figure S13, Supporting Information). Notably, the Na-storage performance of RFSH-1400 electrode surpasses most of the previously reported polymer-derived HCs anode (Table S9, Supporting Information), demonstrating the steric hindrance engineering of the polymer precursors can be a promising strategy to prepare high-performance HC anodes for SIBs. To demonstrate the possibility of the practical application, the full cell constructed by the commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode and RFSH-1400 anode with a capacity ratio 1/1.1 was fabricated (Figure S14, Supporting Information). The full cell can exhibit reversible capacities of 345.6, 299.2, 277.4, 262.9 and 239.6 mAh/g at 0.1, 0.5, 1, 2 and 4 C (based on the mass of active anode material), respectively (Figure S15a–c, Supporting Information). In addition, the full cell can keep a discharge capacity of 260.2 mAh/g with a capacity retention of 82.5 % after 100 cycles at 1 C (Figure S15d, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>To explore the reason for the remarkably enhanced rate performances, the kinetics of the desodiation/sodiation process were estimated by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). According to the previous works,<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0041_R_d82955976e845" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31">31</a></sup></span> the gradual increase of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na+</sub>) in the initial charge process corresponds to the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption on the carbon surface in the slope region (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0005">5f</a>). Subsequently, the “U” shape of D<sub>Na+</sub> in the plateau region is appeared, which is attributed to the conversion of Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism from the intercalation to pore-filling process. It can be clearly observed that RFSH-1400 exhibits a higher D<sub>Na+</sub> than RF-1400, indicating that RFSH-1400 has a more suitable structure for the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport, which can ensure the RFSH-1400 anode to deliver a high plateau capacity at high current density (Figure S16, Supporting Information).<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0044_R_d82955976e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">32</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, the RFSH-1400 electrode has a much smaller charge-transfer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) than that of RF-1400 (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0005">5g</a>). Therefore, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in RFSH-1400 is more convenient owing to the highly disordered graphitic structures and numerous active closed pores, as elucidated in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0005">5h</a>.</p>
         
         <p>The in situ XRD spectra in the sodiation/desodiation processes for RFSH-1400 anode were further performed to clarify Na-storage mechanism. As displayed in Figure S17 (Supporting Information), the intensity of (002) peak experiences a gradual weakening in the discharge process and a continuous increase in the charge process. The decreasing intensities of (002) peak in the sodiation process correspond to the formation of disordered structure caused by the insertion of sodium ions between graphite-like layers, and the it then almost fully recovers in the desodiaton process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0005_R_d82955976e884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett.">3</a></sup></span> More importantly, no obvious shift for the (002) peak can be observed during the charge/discharge process. This is because the (002) plane has enough spacing (0.398 nm) to accommodate sodium ions, which enables the RFSH-1400 to deliver impressive cycle stability at large current density. To verify the occurrence of pore-filling process during the discharge process, the RFSH-1400 electrodes discharged to 0.01 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) were soaked in 1 % phenolphthalein in CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH. As shown in Figure S18 (Supporting Information), the color of the ethanol solution in the RFSH-1400 shows purplish-red, suggesting quasi-metallic Na filling in closed pores.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0045_R_d82955976e893" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Commun.">33</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, the color of the ethanol-phenolphthalein solution with RFSH-1400 shows much deeper than that with RF-1400, indicating RFSH-1400 has more closed pores to store Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>In order to demonstrate the universality of the steric hindrance engineering to prepare high-performance polymer-derived HCs anode, different sterically hindered groups, including biphenyl and 5-methyl-2-furanyl, have been grafted on phenolic resin precursor and then were carbonized at 1400 °C, which were denoted as the RFLB-1400 and RFMF-1400, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0006">6a</a> and Figure S19–S21, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0006">6b</a>, RFLB-1400 and RFMF-1400 anodes exhibit high specific capacities of 334.3 and 321.8 mAh/g, respectively, much higher than that of RF-1400 anode. Similarly, the enhanced sodium storage capacity mainly originates from the plateau capacity, which is attributed to more closed pores for Na<sup>+</sup> filling in RFLB-1400 and RFMF-1400 anodes, as verified by SAXS and TEM (Figure S22–S23, Supporting Information). Besides, RFLB-1400 and RFMF-1400 anodes show greatly enhanced rate performances than RF-1400 anode, and RFLB-1400 anode delivers the most impressive rate capability with a high capacity of 258.9 mAh/g at 5 C (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0006">6c</a>–<a href="#anie202409906-fig-0006">d</a>). Owing to the existence of biphenyl with two benzene ring structures in the RFLB precursor, the as-derived RFLB-1400 possesses higher thermal stability than RFMF-1400 to avoid the over graphitization during the pyrolysis process, and then a larger interlayer spacing can be obtained (Figure S24, Supporting Information), which can ensure faster Na<sup>+</sup> transportation at large current density.<span><sup><a href="#anie202409906-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202409906-bib-0046_R_d82955976e919" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">34</a></sup></span> As expected, RFLB-1400 and RFMF-1400 anodes also deliver superior cycle stability (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0006">6e</a>). Therefore, steric hindrance engineering can be a general strategy to fabricate polymer-derived HC anodes with high sodium storage capacity and rate performance. Specifically, the aromatic ring side group in aldehyde can increase the backbone rigidity as well as the free volume in the polymer precursor. The superior rigidity can suppress the polymer rearrangements and inhibit the over graphitization during the pyrolysis process, while the free volume in the precursor can induce the formation of pores in the HC during the carbonization process (Figure <a href="#anie202409906-fig-0006">6f</a>).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202409906-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d3852783-1d43-4d07-a74a-8be6f5ea8091/anie202409906-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d3852783-1d43-4d07-a74a-8be6f5ea8091/anie202409906-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/637f1cce-5c48-4dd3-9c63-8dd56a84ab8f/anie202409906-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d3852783-1d43-4d07-a74a-8be6f5ea8091/anie202409906-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202409906-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202409906" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Scheme of polymers framework with aromatic ring; (b) The initial discharge/charge curves at 0.1 C and (c) rate performance of RFLB-1400 and RFMF-1400; (d) The comparison of capacity and ICE for different HCs in this wok; (e) The cycling performance of RFLB-1400 and RFMF-1400 at 5 C; (f) Scheme of steric hindrance engineering to modulate the closed pores formation of HC.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202409906-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202409906-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, steric-hindrance effect has been proposed as an efficient strategy to modify the polymer precursor to prepare high-performance HC anode for SIBs for the first time. The experimental data and theoretical calculation show that steric-hindrance effect induced by the aromatic ring side groups on polymers can increase backbone rigidity and the internal free volumes. Then the polymer rearrangements and the over graphitization in the carbonization process can be effectively suppressed, and the free volume in the precursor can facilitate the formation of closed pores in the HC. As a result, the as-obtained HC anode exhibits a high reversible discharge capacity of 340 mAh/g at 0.1 C, impressive rate performance (210 mAh/g at 5 C) and superior cycling stability with a capacity retention ratio of 86.4 % after 1000 cycles at 2 C. Besides, it has been demonstrated that steric hindrance engineering can be a general strategy to prepare high-performance HC anodes from polymer precursors for SIBs. Our work not only enriches the formation mechanism of closed pore, but also provide a new perspective on the design of polymer-derived HC for high-performance SIBs in the future.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
